In all fairness, this product does not claim to cure everything, but it does purport ability to cure everything from toenail fungus to hair loss. Really? Next, in the spirit of honesty, I was sent this without cost in order to assess it's effectiveness. Also, I'd never heard of Tea Tree oil, prior to being offered this opportunity, so I would have to say that I'm in a good position to offer a fair assessment.<br /><br />First -- the smell. Good heavens above, it reeks. It's so off-putting, that a majority of my review is based on research. Actual research from reliable sources (not these overly-enthused five star reviewers). Here's what I discovered.<br /><br />It toxic. If swallowed, it can do some serious harm. Even put someone in a coma. Next, it's a serious irritant. A lot of people who tried using it for toe fungus (again, on medical websites, not here on Amazon), claimed that the smallest concentration caused serious swelling and redness, and that it wasn't effective. Of those reviewing on other sights, the effectiveness for toe fungus was sketchy.<br /><br />Most websites stated that the claims for killing certain bacteria wasn't validated. Those that did claim it killed bacteria, stated it's effectiveness on one type. If anyone has research supporting it's effectiveness, I'll gladly review it. Those who use it in their homes, I'd love to know how you know it's working as an anti-bacterial. Do you go around with a black light and do a before and after. If you just say, \\"I just know\\" then you're claiming something that you don't really know. For those who've had success on things such as lice, when combined with a myriad other ingredients, how do you know that the inclusion of this oil was the deciding factor of that success over the combination and/or a single other ingredient? I'm not certain that so much success should be attributed to this oil when I've read so manyreviews stating that it is used sparingly in conjunction with other things.<br /><br />Anyway, here's something that I found for those who are interested in knowing a little more facts about this product: For me, personally, I won't be buying it and am glad that I didn't pay for it. The claims are too far-fetched for me and the smell is horrible.<br /><br />TEA TREE OIL Uses & Effectiveness<br /><br />Possibly Effective for:<br /><br /> Athlete's foot (tinea pedis). Topical application of a 10% tea tree oil cream works about as well as tolnaftate 1% cream (Genaspor, Tinactin, Ting, and others) for relieving symptoms of athlete's foot, including scaling, inflammation, itching, and burning. However, the 10% tea tree oil cream doesn't seem to cure the infection. A stronger tea tree oil solution (25% or 50%) is needed for that. Application of 25% or 50% tea tree oil solution appears to both relieve symptoms and clear up the infection in about half of people who try it for 4 weeks. However, 25% or 50% tea tree oil concentrations don't appear to be as effective for curing the infection as medications such as clotrimazole or terbinafine.<br /> Fungus infections of the nails (onychomycosis). Topical application of 100% tea tree oil solution, twice daily for six months, can cure fungal toenail infection in about 18% of people who try it. It can also improve nail appearance and symptoms in about 56% of patients after three months and 60% of patients after six months of treatment. It seems to be comparable to twice daily application of clotrimazole 1% solution (Fungoid, Lotrimin, Lotrimin AF). Lower concentrations of tea tree oil do not seem to be as effective. For example, there is some evidence that a 5% tea tree oil cream applied three times daily for two months has no benefit.<br /> Mild to moderate acne. Applying a 5% tea tree oil gel appears to be as effective as 5% benzoyl peroxide (Oxy-5, Benzac AC, and others) for treating acne. Tea tree oil might work more slowlythan benzoyl peroxide, but seems to be less irritating to facial skin. When applied twice daily for 45 days, tea tree oil reduces several acne symptoms, including acne severity.<br /><br />Insufficient Evidence for:<br /><br /> Bad breath. Early research shows that adding tea tree oil to an essential oil mixture containing peppermint and lemon oils can reduce bad breath.<br /> Cold sores (Herpes labialis). Research so far suggests that applying 6% tea tree oil gel 5 times daily does not significantly improve cold sores.<br /> Eyelid infection (blepharitis). Early research shows that tea tree might cure common eyelid infections and reduce the associated symptoms, including eye inflammation and vision loss.<br /> Dandruff. Early research suggests that applying a 5% teat tree oil shampoo three minutes daily for four weeks reduces scalp lesions, scalp itchiness, and greasiness in patients with dandruff.<br /> Dental plaque. Results from research examining the effects of tea tree oil on dental plaque are inconsistent. Some early research shows that brushing the teeth with a 2.5% tea tree oil gel twice daily for eight weeks reduces gum bleeding but not plaque in people who have gingivitis caused by plaque. Also, using a mouthwash containing tea tree oil after a professional teeth cleaning does not seem to reduce plaque formation. However, rinsing with a specific product (Tebodont) containing tea tree oil and a chemical called xylitol does seem to reduce plaque.<br /> Gingivitis. Results from research examining the effects of tea tree oil on gingivitis are inconsistent. Some early research shows that brushing the teeth with a 2.5% tea tree oil gel twice daily for eight weeks reduces gum bleeding but does not improve overall gum health in people who have gingivitis caused by plaque. However, rinsing with a specific product (Tebodont) containing tea tree oil and a chemical called xylitol seems to reduce gum inflammation.<br /> Hemorrhoids. Some early evidence suggeststhat applying a gel containing tea tree oil reduces symptoms of hemorrhoids, including pain, inflammation and itching, in children.<br /> Lice. Early research suggests that tea tree oil might repel lice. Also, applying a combination of lavender and tea tree oil kills lice eggs and reduces the number of live lice. It is unclear if the effects are caused by the tea tree oil alone or the combination of lavender and tea tree oil.<br /> Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus (MRSA) infection. Evidence about the effects of tea tree oil on MRSA infection is unclear. When compared to standard MRSA treatment alone, adding tea tree oil does not seem to improve overall MRSA clearance. Also, using a tea tree oil solution when cleaning wounds does not seem to improve MRSA clearance. However, an early study suggests that using a 4% tea tree oil nasal ointment plus a 5% tea tree oil body wash along with other standard treatments might have a small benefit.<br /> Allergic skin reactions to nickel. Developing evidence suggests that undiluted tea tree oil may reduce the area and redness skin reactions in people who are allergic to contact with nickel. Also some early evidence suggests that applying diluted tea tree oil to the skin before nickel exposure reduces skin reactions in people allergic to nickel.<br /> Water warts. Early research shows that applying a combination of tea tree oil and iodine for 30 days helps clear up warts in children better than just tea tree oil or iodine alone.<br /> Yeast infections in the mouth and throat (thrush; also known as oropharyngeal candidiasis). Because people with AIDS have a weak immune system, they sometimes come down with \\"opportunistic\\" infections such as thrush. There is some evidence that tea tree oil might be beneficial in patients with HIV/AIDS whose thrush does not respond to usual antifungal medications such as fluconazole. Swishing and expelling tea tree oil solution for two to four weeks seems to improve symptoms. Thereis also early evidence that using tea tree oil as part of an essential oil mixture might improve oral comfort in cancer patients. However, adding 1 mL of tea tree oil to a standard conditioner treatment does not reduce infections and inflammation in people with dentures.<br /> Vaginal bacterial infections. Some studies suggest tea tree oil may be beneficial for some vaginal infections caused by bacteria.<br /> Vaginal yeast infections. Early evidence suggests that tea tree oil might be beneficial for some vaginal yeast infections.<br /> Ear infections.<br /> Scabies.<br /> Ringworm.<br /> Preventing infections in cuts, abrasions, burns, insect bites and stings, and boils.<br /> Sore throat.<br /> Cough.<br /> Congestion.<br /> Other conditions.<br /><br />More evidence is needed to rate tea tree oil for these uses.<br /><br />AMENDED 2/11/2015<br /><br />I was attempting to warn purchasers without slamming the product too much, as reaction may vary; however, I was called on my statement that a majority of my review was based on research. It is true that I was attempting to allow the research to speak for itself, but we DID attempt to use this product. As I don't want people to get the wrong impression, I will post here that which I responded to the individual who declared I should have tried it first. Here was our actual experience with this product. Again, this may work fine for some people, but people should really be careful before assuming too much. Again -- here was my response to the gentleman in question:<br /><br />\\"My apologies, but I said \\"a majority of my review is based on research\\". I should have clarified that my husband and I tried the product in three different capacities. First, I attempted to use it as an antibacterial spray, despite absolutely NO evidence that it is actually effective at killing germs (other than staph, which is questionable). My house reeked for hours afterward. So, attempt number one was disastrous.<br /><br />My husband has toe fungus on one toe. Persistent and aggravating. \\"Let me try it on the toe fungus.\\" Again, despite absolutely NO evidence that this is effective, he gave it a go. It immediately caused a negative reaction. I was smart enough NOT to put it in the shampoo container, rather placed a tiny bit on the shampoo in my hand. I got a migraine afterward. I know my shampoo didn't cause the migraine, and it occurred right after my shower. Strike three.\\"

Tea Tree oil has been used a myriad of ways.<br /><br />Some uses are:<br /><br />Skin care<br />First Aid<br />Household cleaning<br />Hair & Scalp care<br />Aromatherapy<br />Feminine care<br />Chronic Illnesses<br />Dental Care<br /><br />Traditionally the Bundjalung Aborigines would crush up the tea tree leaves and inhale them for treatment of the common cold or symptoms such as coughing and wheezing. Crushed tea tree leaves were often rubbed directly into a wound, to disinfect and assist with the healing process. More commonly however, the leaves were soaked and used to treat skin ailments, wounds and even sore throats. Soaked leaves were the most common, and likely the most effective, way to use the Melaleuca tree for medicinal purposes. An interesting story about the Aborigines‘ use of the tea tree for healing is that of the healing lakes used by the Bundjalung. It is unknown if they knew it at the time, but research and the oral history indicate the 'healing lakes‘ were in fact lagoons filled with tea tree leaves. These leaves had naturally fallen into the lakes from nearby trees, decayed, and essentially fortified the water with their healing properties, over time.<br /><br />The first thing that I wanted to try using the Tea Tree oil for was hair and scalp care. Some known benefits of using tea tree oil for your hair are; Prevents hair loss, treats lice, facilitates hair growth, treats & eliminates dandruff, treats cradle cap and also unblocks the hair follicles that cause dandruff. [[VIDEOID:dd08ded0a85dbc8e9b560955599f3f27]]If you've gone to any natural health and beauty store recently, you may have noticed tea tree oil as a prominent ingredient on all sorts of personal hygiene products, like shampoos, soaps, mouthwash, and [[ASIN:B005P0X2Q4 Desert Essence Toothpaste Tea Tree Neem-Wintergreen]]. Despite its name, the tea tree is not at all related to the tea that we drink (Camellia sinensis), but is a member of the myrtle family and is known by its scientific name Melaleuca alternifolia. And being natural, of course, is nothing new except to us silly over-industrialized folk who have filled our homes with cheap chemicals and mass-produced toxic cleaning products and lost touch with Mother Nature. Indigenous peoples in Australia have been using the oils from its leaves for centuries to treat coughs and other ailments.<br /><br />The product comes in a beautifully designed cardboard box that references artwork from health products of the 1800's. Tea tree oil, because of its antiseptic and disinfecting properties, has many uses for personal hygiene, skincare, and household cleaning. While I've used products containing tea tree oil for years, I've never actually seen or used tea tree oil in its pure form. The oil itself is clear and comes in a brown glass bottle to protect it from light. It is made in Australia and contains nothing else. The odor is quite strong and very distinctive, smelling like a combination of camphor, rosemary, limes, pine needles, and old wet dish rags. It can be used alone as a wipe or combined with other ingredients to make lotions, balms, and home cleaning products like dryer sheets, toilet cleaners, oven cleaner.
